Prayer helped kidnap victim through ordeal 

By CECIL CLIFT 
A young San Antonian relied upon his religion to help him survive a 16-hour drive to New 
Mexico at gunpoint. 
“ I prayed quite a bit of the time all the way out there." Sieve Smith, 23, said. 
Smith made the comment Monday in a press conference at police h ead ­ quarters as he recalled how he was abducted by a “ custom er" Thursday night from the North Side sporting goods store where he worked. 
Smith was released unharmed by his abductor near Capitan. N M., over the weekend. 
The gunman then went on to kidnap three other persons and attempted to rob two service stations before filially being shot to death by Colorado 
Springs. Colo., police, investigators said. 
Police identified the dead man as 
Jerry Wayne Alston, 29. of 7931* Blanco 
Road. He had moved back to San An­ tonio three m onths ago after being paroled from a W ashington sta te prison. Av here he was serving a 2(1 year sentence for arm ed robbery, police said.

Court keeps fans out 

Country entertainer Webb Pierce put a sign at the foot of his driveway, in Nashville telling tour­ ists it s a court injunction, riot inhospitality, that keeps them out. 
Pierce is well known for inviting fans lo see his 1o-acre estate and that guitar-shaped swim m ing pool. 
“ It's really against my constitu­ tional rights.” said Pierce, who is appealing a Chancery Court ruling last September that said activities at his Curtiswood Lane home “ are commercial in nature and in viola­ tion of the ordinances of the City of 
Oak Hill,” the posh resid ential neighborhood where he lives. 
The a p p e a l is s c h e d u l e d for hearing May IO. The city contends 
Pierce violated the law by inviting the public and selling record al­ bums

R e p u b l i c a n l e a d e r J o h n R hodes startled his colleagues, therefore, bv flinging m ud at Speaker T hom as P. 
O'Neill Jr. last year. 
Now it has turned out that Rhodes 

 w a s not without sin after all. 
He attacked Tip O'Neill iii a book that was largely written bv a House employe. 
Thus the taxpayers subsidized the scathing profce. but odes pocketed the royalties. 
Book 
T he a f o r e s a id book. “ T he F u tile 
S y stem ,” p ortra ys O ’Neill unflatter- bigly as a blustering partisan. 
This deeply offended O'Neill who expressed his indignation characteris­ tically with a roar of outrage from the 
House floor. 
The short, dapper Rhodes played the m a t a d o r to th e s n o r t i n g , bull-like 
O'Neill. “ The gentleman from Massa­ ch usetts." responded Rhodes deftly, 
“ will find some very interesting read­ ing in that b o o k " 
Now it is the Speaker e turn to call 
Rhodes’ attention to some interesting reading. 
Fo r we have learned that Rhodes used a g h o s t w r i t e r n a m e J . B ria n 
Sm ith to put the book into literary form. Rhodes dictated the material; then Smith rewrote it. 
The talented Smith was paid, how­ ever, not by Rhodes but by the ta x ­ payers. 
Smith appears on the public payroll as a $31.ooo-a-year re ­ cipient of the taxpayers' la r­ gesse. 
Yet R hodes collected the 
Tull $8,ooo in royalties. 
_________ 
Smith told us that his boss g e n e ro u s ly offered him p art of th e royalties. 
'rile g h o s t w r i t e r t u r n e d down the offer p resum a bly because he had al­ ready been paid a d e q u a t e ly by th e taxpayers. 
Question 
T h i s r a i s e s an i n t e r e s t i n g legal question. 
It is against the law for a congress­ m a n to t a k e k i c k b a c k s f ro m his employes. lf instead he takes the proceeds from an employe’s public service, does this constitute a kickback? 
In an anguished interview. Smith insisted there had been no improprie­ ty. 
He acknowledged that he had worked on the book during office hours, but be said he had also labored over it even­ ings and weekends. “ I put in my 4o hours for the governm ent," he said. 
There is more to the story. Smith has a sideline; he is a 5o per cent partner in the public relations firm of Smith and 
Harroff. 
Rhodes retained the firm last year to handle the advertising for his re-elec­ tion campaign. 
Firm 
For its efforts, the firm was paid 
$12,5oo in fees and commissions from the c o n g r e s s m a n 's c a m p a ig n collec­ tions. 
Sm ith has ren o u n ced any in come from the firm while he is working for the g o v e r n m e n t , but he still has a stake in its welfare. 
For half of the com pany’s assets will revert to him when he leaves the fe­ deral payroll. 
Both Rhodes and Smith contend that their actions didn’t violate the House ethics code in effect last year or the tougher new code adopted last week. 
This depends, of course, on the defini­ tion of a kickback. 
Too much advice 
Our federal administrators receive altogether too much advice, m ost of it bud, from a bewildering assortment of advisory committees. 
President J im m y ( arter promised to 

Jack 
Anderson 

 clear out s o m e of this b u r e a u c r a ti c underbrush if he made it to the White 
House. 
Within 3o da y s of bis a r r i v a l , lie created four new advisory committees. 
This disturbs the Senate Subcom­ m ittee on Reports, Accounting and 
Management, which has tried to keep a check on advisory committees. 
The s u b c o m m i t t e e found 81 c o m ­ mittees, for example, advising the go­ vernm ent what to do about energy. 
These co m m ittees w ere split into a multitude of subcommittees and task forces, all contributing advice on the same subject. 
The total num ber of advisory units, according to a careful count, exceed 
25o. 

Smith appears on the public payrolls as a S313IOO a year recipient o f the taxpayers largesse. 

The advice that com es from these 
25o units, however, isn't as confusing and conflicting as might be supposed. 
For the Senate investigators found that the governm ent had loaded the c o m m ittee s with ad v iso rs from the energy-industrial complex. 
Not s u r p r i s i n g l y , th e s e a d v i s o r s agree overwhelmingly upon measures that would benefit the industry. 
Apparently, it m akes no difference that the law requires the m e m bership on a d v i s o r y c o m m i t t e e s to be b a ­ lanced. all points of view are supposed to lie represented. 
In a report not yet released to tho public, the subcommittee notes wryly; 
"T h e requirem ent for balance is, at best, subjected to widely varying in­ terpretations." 
For example, 38 advisors have been appointed to advise the Federal Power 
Commission on the impact of the na­ tural gas shortage. 
“ A p p a r e n t l y . ” s t a t e s th e re p o r t, 
“ the only consumers being considered by th is c o m m i t t e e w ere i n d u s t r i a l consumers.*’ 
The investigators found only one a p ­ pointee from a public interest group; the other 35 cam e from corporations or government agencies. 
Consumers 
The few consum er representatives who are admitted to the advisory circle are seldom heeded. 
The executive advisory co m m ittee on the National Gas Survey, for e x a m ­ ple. brought in some public-interest people. 
But the advisors, according to the report, “ were urged to keep ‘dissent­ ing o p i n i o n s ’ w hich m i g h t e m e r g e from ‘the diversity of m e m b e rsh ip ’ to a m in im u m .” 
The advisors who exam ine energy policy questions invariably come from such corporations as Exxon and Con­ solidated Edison. Exxon was r e p r e ­ sented on 15 ad v i s o ry c o m m i t t e e s , 
Consolidated Edison on IO . 
The total bill for all this dubious advice, according to the best available estimate, may run the taxpayers over 
$2o million a year.

Al SH N — Lo-Vaca’s participation in interstate gas transportation won't interfere with San Antonio’s plans to sell gas to the Northeast, the Texas 
Railroad Commission said Monday. 
The commission unanimously ap­ proved a resolution intended to relieve the city's concerns it may not be able to use Lo-Vaca's pipeline system to 
" transport gas to cities in the Northeast. 
San Antonio City Public Service officials say if possible, the city plans to help hardpressed cities in the 
Northeast by selling some of its own gas. 

The city's electric generating plants would use fuel oils to replace whatever gas can be sold. 
Any sale, however, would be inter­ ruptive if there is any sign of a gas curtailment in San Antonio, said Rail­ road Commission Chairman Mack 
Wallace. 
“ One of the conditions of our state­ ment of no objection is that there would be no curtailments locally.’’ said Wallace. 
CPS Gen. Mgr. Jack Spruce said no firm negotiations have begun, but several Eastern gas companies have 

 inquired about the availability of gas from San Antonio's system. 
Equitable Gas Co. from Pittsburgh, 
Pa. has shown the most interest. 
Spruce said. 
Price 

A member of CPS legal staff said the city’s stand is any gas sold to the 
Northeast would not be sold any cheaper than the price San Antonio is paying for it. 

According to Spruce, the city would buy gas from Lo-Vaca for movement to the Northeast. 

The city would then be reimbursed for the purchase of alternate fuels, 
Spruce said. 

 lf any future gas sales are arranged. 
Spruce said the city could afford to send up to 4o million cubic feet of gas daily on an intermittent basis 

Commissioners commended San An­ tonio for volunteering to provide gas to others in need of it. 

The city w ould be participating on a volunteer basis under provisions of the 
President Carter's Emergency Natural 
Gas Act.

Gambling bill faces buzz saw; 
Pyndus hit from all sides 

Don’t take too seriously those glow ing predictions about Texas legalizing pari­ mutuel betting. 

An Associated Press article in 
Monday’s Express suggested that 
1977 could be the Year of the Horse in the Lone Star State. 
In the article, House Speaker Bill 
Clayton was quoted as saying that a bill legalizing horse race betting on a local option basis “ has a fair chance of passage this session.’’ 
Don’t believe it. 
The chances of the state's legis­ lators putting their political careers on the line for the horse racing lobby arc the same as they’ve al­ ways been — slim to none. 
Suicide 
A vote for legalized gambling would be political suicide in many legislative districts, and scions know it. 
The religious groups — notably 
Southern Baptists — which oppose pari-mutuel betting arc strong, po­ litically sophisticated and highly emotional about the issue. 
They represent a potential elee- tion-dav buzz saw that few legisla­ tors want to tangle with. 
In San Antonio, that buzz saw is led by Rev. Jiminy Allen, who pre­ dicts: 
“ The people of Texas have de­ feated legalized gambling before, and they will do it again.’’ 
Rev. Allen, the pastor of First 
Baptist Church, says he is weary of 
“ wealthy sportsmen and their compatriots trying to push through legislation legalizing betting on a sport that has such a negative im­ pact on society. 
“ The people who arc victimized by tilt' dream of quick answers to poverty are the people who can least afford to gamble. 
“ I hope the legislators act re­ sponsibly and reject this bill.” 
Whether they accept or reject it. the effort to legalize horse race betting will undoubtedly be one of the hottest issues during this legis­ lative session. 
Independent Phil 
City Councilman Phil Pyndus is learning that independence doesn't always pay. 
Many 
City 
Hull observers consider 
Pyndus the most independent (and some say the most honest) politician around 
For the past two years he has consistently refused to bend to any pressure other than his own cons­ 

 cience on matters before the City 
Council. 
While such integrity might help him sleep well. it hasn't made him too many friends among the lobby groups at City Hall 
For instance, while Pyndus is a conservative Republican and pro­ bably the biggest booster of the free enterprise system on the council, the town's business establishment hasn’t rushed to his banner in the current council campaign. 
Alliance 
Indeed, the Alliance for a Better 
City — that new moneyed political group with all the old Good Go­ vernment League faces — seems bent on booting Pyndus out of of­ fice. 
Word is out that the ABC will back I) Ford Nielsen against Pyn­ dus in the District 8 race. 
Councilman Nielsen, once the darling of the town’s liberals, has made a 18o-degree turn the past two years and is now the sweetheart of 
Greater San Antonio Chamber of 
Commerce types and North Side developers. 
He has consistently championed their causes at City Hall while 
Pyndus has followed an indepen­ dent course. 
Power brokers 
Ironically, while getting in trou­ ble with the business community’s power brokers. Pyndus has also managed to make the town’s labor unions mad (They don’t like inde­ pendent politicians either.) 
Sheet Metal Workers Local Union 
No. 87 has encouraged its member to actively campaign against Pyn­ dus. 
In a recent newsletter, tin4 union's business manager. R. T. Stringer, denounced Pyndus for opposing an attempt to have union rates de­ clared the prevailing wage on (Tty of 
San 
Antonio construction projects. 
Pyndus contended that the union rates were higher than prevailing wages and would adversely affect the city's coffers. 
His efforts on behalf of the town’s taxpayers earned him the wrath of the unions. Stringer wrote his sheet metal workers: “ Let’s try to keep 
Mr Pyndus from being re-elected.” 
So as election day approaches, the councilman finds himself attacked 
.from all sides by lobby groups that have tried unsuccessfully to put him iii their bag. 
On April 2. they’ll try to teach him that independence carries a

Under its interim order, however, the commission said money Lo-Vaca makes from transporting gas out of state will continue to be held in escrow until it is determined how it can best be spent. 
As a result, the commission said it w ill keep a hold on around $1.6 million 
Lo-Vaca is in the process of making as a pipeline link in moving emergency gas supplies from Texas to tho energy short Northeast. 

 under provisions of the Emergency 
Natural Gas Act recently signed into law by President Jimmy Carter.

 least from intrastate gas transporta­ tion charges. 
Initially, the commission said it is­ sued the freeze order because of re­ ports Lo-Vaca would make windfall profits from transporting gas to hard­ 

 pressed Northeastern cities. 
.Under Monday’s interim order, 
Lo-Vaca is required to report its inter­ state and intrastate gas transportation revenues to the commission each month. 

Revenues

* spending any money the company makes from transporting gas inside or out of the state until a complete ae% counting could be made of the re­ venues. 
In a subsequent hearing. Lo-Vaca attorneys responded with a threat to sue the commission for interfering in 
V the movement of emergency gas supplies badly needed in the North­ east. 
A Lo-Vaca official said Monday a detailed study will be made of the commission’s interim order to deter­ mine whether to follow through with the suit. 
At last month's hearing, Lo-Vaca 
President Bill Greehey said the com­ pany could not survive through April 
' without use of the transportation re­ venues to pay its own bills to gas pro­ ducers.

Cisneros: Resales of CPS gas should benefit consumers 

By JIM WOOD 
Mayor Pro Tem Henry 
Cisneros suggested Mon­ day that the city’s utility consider getting into the gas resale businees in a big way with benefits go­ ing to consumers. 
His comments followed an order by the Texas 
Railroad Commission al­ low mg 
City 
Public 
Service to make emer­ gency gas sales to northeastern states by transferring its own gas and burning fuel oil in­ stead. 
UPS generators burn natural gas to produce electricity but have the capability to burn fuel oil as well as gas. 
Oil 
Until now UPS has held the fuel oil in reserve to use in case the gas sup­ plier. Lo-Vaea Gathering 
Co., could not meet its quotas. 
Utsueros said gas sold out of state, under emer­ gency federal authority. is commanding up to 
$2.35 per I.(HK) cubic feet and the equivalent cost of fuel oil is $2. 
By making more use of the cheaper fuel oil and 

 selling the gas, Cisneros said CPS stands to make an extra $5 million a year. 

“ Such a sum would produce significant customer benefits if cre­ dited directly to the fuel account and then to customers’ monthly bills.’* Cisneros said in a letter to City Mgr. Tom 
Huebner and UPS Gener­ al Mgr. Jack Spruce. 
Storage 
Cisneros said the key to making more sales is the ability of CPS to store the oil. CPS would need a storage capacity of some 
2 million barrels to consider using oil as a primary fuel and not just for backup, he said. 

He asked for a report on storage capacity and cost of adding to it. 

CPS gas will he trans­ ferred to other suites through Lo-Vaea pipe­ lines. Lo-Vaca’s capa­ cities will determine how much gas can actually be sold out of state.

Suspected bank robber linked to Harlandale heist 

SA N A N T O N IO EXPRESS— -Tuesday March 8 1977 

The man charged with rob­ bing Brooks Field National 
Bank allegedly paid for a ge­ taway car with money stolen from Harlandale State Bank about two weeks earlier. 

Roberto Ramirez, 45, address unk­ nown, was charged Monday with the 
Feb. 14 robbery of Brooks Field Na­ tional. 
He is in Bexar County .Jail in lieu of a 
$25o,ooo bond set by U.S. Magistrate 

Joseph F. Leonard Jr. 
Ramirez was shot in the mouth by 
FBI agents Feb. 18 as the agents sought to apprehend him in Austin. 
Another man, Jesse Godinez of Aus­ tin. has been charged with bank rob­ bery for allegedly driving the getaway car in the Brooks Field robbery. 
He is in Bexar County Jail in lieu of 
$5o,ooo bond. 
No charges have been filed in con­ nection with the Jan. 2fi Harlandale bank robbery, which netted $18,ooo. 

After the Brooks Field robbery, a complaint filed by the FBI alleges, the getaway car was burned and its occu­ pants fled to Austin. 
The car was bought by two men for 
$25o from a San Antonio used car dealer, the complaint charges. 
The cash given had serial numbers matching bills taken from Harlandale. the FBI agents charged. 
A teller at the bank told FBI agents the robber came to her window and said, “ Give me all your money or vouTl die; I’m not kidding,” the com­ 

 plaint charged. 
He then told another teller, “ Give me all your money quick or IT I kiil you,” agents charged. 
A bank official chased the robbers after they left the bank, but turned back after one of them took a shot at him, the agents stated in the com ­ plaint. 
Ju d ge Leonard scheduled a preli­ minary examination for Ramirez next 
Monday. 
Ramirez was brought from Austin to 
San Antonio for his court appearance.

Court opens doors to state tax millions 

WASHINGTON (AP) - In separate rulings Monday, the Supreme Court opened the door for states to collect untold millions in tax revenues from outof-state companies and said judges cannot block a newspaper from pub­ lishing information obtained at a pub­ lic hearing. 
The court said in a unanimous deci­ sion that states have the power to tax a company for the privilege of doing bu­ siness if any portion of the company's operation is carried out exclusively within the state. 
“ No doubt many state legislatures will be rushing through a pack of new laws sparked by the court’s decision,” said Jam es ll. Haddock, the lawyer who successfully defended a M issis­ sippi tax law before the high court. 
“ There's a lot of money to be made from this newly recognized state power,” Haddock said. “ This is a major victory for the states.” 
In its first statement on freedom of tlu* press since a much publicized de­ cision last June, the court unanimously refused to let judges make exceptions to newspapers’ right to publish infor­ 

 mation obtained at a public hearing. 
The court ruled O klahom a state courts abridged the constitutional righ ts of three 
Oklahom a City newspapers in issuing a “ gag order” to ban publication of certain informa­ tion about a c a se involving an 
11-year-old boy charged in a shooting death. 
The boy’s name and picture were widely displayed after a preliminary hearing, prompting a county judge to order them to cease publicity because of the boy’s right to rehabilitation. 
The three papers, the Daily Oklaho­ man, the Oklahoma City Times and the 
Sunday Oklahoman, appealed. 
The high court agreed, ruling that a judge cannot suppress news gathered in a public hearing even if that hearing legally could have been closed. The county judge in the case. Charles Hai­ ley, said after the decision, “ The judgment tells us what we must do in the future is see that juvenile pro­ ceedings are closed from the outset if we are to succeed in avoiding publicity such as happened in this case.

( AIRO — Saudi Arabia announced an unexpect­ ed pledge of $1 billion for 
African developm ent 
Monday as leaders of KO 
Arab and African nations opened their first sum ­ mit meeting in Cairo. 
Prolonged ap p lau se greeted the announce­ ment by Saudi Foreign 
Minister Prince Saudi El 
Faisal and more clapping followed each detail of the’contribution from the 
Arab w orld’s rich est country. 
Sadat stressed African backing for the A rab cause against Israel, in exchange for Arab sup­ port for liberation move­ ments fighting in south­ ern Africa. 
Summit solidarity was sligh tly cracked, however, by Ugandan 
President Idi Amin, who m ade indirect threats again st neighboring 
Kenya, which he has ac­ cused of harboring forces intended to invade Ugan* da. 
Amin said he had done nothing about attack s and threats of invasion for the sake of African, 
Arab and world unity, but warned he could not

“ second m ir a c le ” pressed the search for five men trapped inside a flooded-out coal mine but expected to find no other air pockets like that winch saved miner Ron­ ald Ad ley.

Carter to attend summit meets 

WASHINGTON - Pre­ sident Carter plans to at­ tend two overseas sum ­ mit meetings later this year. White House Press 
Secretary Judy Powell said. 
Powell said Carter, as expected, will participate in an international meet­ ing focusing on economic matters. In addition, he said, the President plans to attend a second sum ­ mit involving leaders of the member nations of the North Atlantic Treaty 
Organization.

WASHINGTON (A P) 
— President ( arter will announce the long-ex­ pected appointment of 
John White us deputy secretary of agriculture 
“ very soon,” a White 
House personnel official said Monday. 
White, T e x a s ’s co m ­ missioner of agriculture, has been in line for the number two spot at the departm ent for more than a month. Hts a p ­ pointment has been delayed by the extensive background chet ks, fin­ ancial disclosure docum ents and other item s required by the 
Carter administration. 
White himself, reached at hts office in Austin. said he had not been for­ mally notified of the ap­ pointment But the White 
House iHTsonnel official, 
Diana Rock, said “ noth­ ing su b stan tiv e ” remained to he checked in hts case. 
She said thai it would 
Im* up to the Agriculture 
Department to formally 

 pass the word along to 
White. 
Carter did announce another m ajor appointment at the agriculture department 
Monday. He named Carol 
Tucker Foreman to the new job of assistan t secretary for food and consumer services. 
White, 52. was only 26 when he first won elec­ tion as state agriculture com m issioner in 195o, defeating incumbent J E. 
McDonald. A native of 

Clay County in northwest 
Texas he was born on a small tenant farm near 
Newport. Attended rural schools and graduated from Iowa Park High 
School in 1942, earning a sch olarship to T ex as 
Tech where received de­ gree in agricu ltu re in 
1946. 
White was state chair­ man of the Lloyd Bentsen campaign in 
1978 primaries, then switched to the Carter camp after 
Bentsen lost out.

Oil production high forecast 

Texas is fspringy’ 

Spring-like weather returned to Texas Monday with plenty of sunshine and pleasant temperatures over most of the state. The only exception was the 
Bio Grande V alley where a thin cover of clouds remained 
Winds were light northeasterly over Southeast 
Texas and along the Coast. Light west to southwes­ terly winds were spreading dry air across West and 
North Texas. 
Temperature readings climbed rapidly across the state in tin* morning reaching the atis and Bbs before noon. 
The National Weather Service said fair skies and warm temperatures would continue through 
Tuesday. 

COMPILED FROM WIRE SERVICES 
TULSA — World pro­ duel ion of crude oil is likely to set another all- time high this year, the 
Oil and Gas Journal re­ ports in its latest edition. 
Total world production climbed 7.9 per cent last year for a new record of just under 57.3 million barrels per day, the weekly business maga­ zine said. 
Industry specialists in­ terviewed by the maga­ zine foresee1 a modest production gain again this year, perhaps by as much as 4 to 5 per cent in mm-Communist areas. 
Most specialists told the magazine that the in­ crease will come from outside the Organization of Petroleum Exporting 
Countries, principally from the North Sea and the Alaskan North Slope. 

PUC suggests lower increase 
AUSTIN - The Public 
Utility Commission staff recommended Monday that Texas Power and 
Light Co. receive less than half of the $134 mil­ lion the company is seeking in rate increases, 
In prefiled testimony. 
PUC Chief Accountant 
Thomas L. Boudreaux said TP&L has a revenue deficiency til $82.9 mil­ ium compared with the company's request for more than $134.8 million. 
The PUC staff said the company, which serves more than 5o counties in 
North and Central Texas, actually is requesting about $144 million, since 

Boudreaux said he has added $9.3 million to re­ place a downward ad­ justment in TP&L’s fuel costs. 

Oil transport plan announced 

MINNEAPOLIS - Two oil companies have an­ nounced a plan to tran­ sport oil from the West 
Coast to the Upper Mid­ west . 
Atlantic Richfield Co. and Trans Mountain Pipe 
Lines Co. announced a plan to deliver Alaskan and foreign oil to Puget 
Sound and move it eastward using existing 
TransMountainpipelines in Edmonton, Alberta. . 
Trans Mountain Presi­ dent Kenn Hall said the proposed pipeline could provide about 18o,ooo to 
2oo.ooo barrels a day to 
Montana, 
Wyoming, 
North 
Dakota, 
Minnesota, Wisconsin, and Western Michigan refiners. 

(iulf & W estern to buy Garden 

NEW YORK - Gulf & 
Western Industries Inc., the largest stockholder in 
Madison Square Garden, announced plans Monday to buy the entire New 
York City sports complex for about $29 million. 
Gulf & Western, which owns 39 per cent of the 
Garden, or 1.877 million 

 shares, said it would pay 
$1o a share for the re­ maining 
2.9 million shares of the Garden's common stock. 
The Garden’s stock was selling at $9.75 a share Monday, up from 
$5 on Friday. 
Union 
O Ks pact 

Union members at In­ gram Mfg. Co. have ap­ proved a new two-year contract, according to 
Paul B. Javior, a union field representative. 
The new contract, with the International Union of Electrical, Radio and 
Machine Workers. AFL- 
CIO, Local 1o13 and representing some 2oo employes, calls for im­ proved economic bene­ fits. 
The improvements in­ clude more holidays, more break periods and a total of $1.1o increase in wages over the life of the contract. 
The new contract will begin the average rate for employes to $5 plus per hour during the term of the contract, said Ja ­ vior. 
The Ingram facility is located at 54o C’ulebra 
Ave.

Salvation possible 
F. Ray Marshall, LLS. secretary til labor, said he believes it is possible to re-establish family farms, but he is not sure it will be done 
Marshall revealed some of his hopes for his department during a press conference before he spoke at the 
Farmers Union convention Sunday. 
On the subject of migrant labor, he said the department hopes to improve the lot of Ihe laborers, 
He expressed the need for better housing, better working condition and better educational opportunities tor the children of migrant workers. 
Marshall also told the newsmen the migrant workers should have an op­ portunity to train for non-farm jobs or lo become farmers themselves. 
During the coni'erence he said lie did not believe a bracero program would be good in that it brought the best workers from another country and put 
(hem in competition with U.S. families paving taxes. 
Marshall also expressed his opposi­ tion to right to work laws on the grounds that such laws do not promote collective bargaining

Mayor, 
East 
Siders talk 

More than 7o East Side residents, mostly from the Henderson-Artesia 
Village area, made their community improvement needs known to Mayor 
Lila Cockrell Monday evening. 
The citizens, members of Residents Organized 
For Better and Beautiful 
Environmental Develop­ ment, met with Mayor 
Cockrell and city staff members at St. Phillip's 
Episcopal Church. 131o 
Pecan Valley Drive 
ROBBED m em bers asked for help with va­ c a n t lots, bad streets and a lack of drainage and sidewalks. 
Drainage 

M ayor Cockrell said the two needed drainage projects could hest be funded as capital im ­ provements in a bond program. 
However. 
ROBBED mem ber 
M rs. 
.Joyce 
McCullen said other funds must be found be­ cause many drainage projects funded by bonds remain unbuilt for more than IO years. 
M ayor Cockrell ad vised the group to seek support for the drainage projects in next year's funds from the 
Community Development 
Aet. 
She added that if sur­ plus CD A funds become available, council may switch those toward the community's needs.

City Council will meet with 
City W ater Board trustees 
Tuesday ti* iron out differences over water service extension jMtliiies. 
The disagreem ent stems from a council resolution passed last summer 
The resolution instructed 
CWB to limit use of the Com­ munity W ater Development fund to within city limits. 
The 
( W D C is a 
( WB-operuted fund providing 
I front money to housing de­ velopers to extend w ater mains. 
Council critics of the fund have said it is a subsidy to 
I developers and creates unbu- j lanced and costly growth on the 
| North Side. 
Policy changed 

CWH has nut changed its po* j la y to reflect the council reso* 
I ut ion. 
CWB 
Chairm an 
.lo tin 
' Schaefer has defended the fund 
I as a good planning tool fur the 
I utility's expansion 
Also, (A V B a tto r n e y 
H u b e r t 

Sawtcllc bas written un opinion stating the council resolution may be illegal because it dis­ crim inates between city customers and those residing outside city limits. 

Since council has passed the resolution, CWB trustees have approved use of the CW DF outside city limits twice, to the 
Western Hills subdivision, one mile north of the University of 
Texas at San .Antonio, and to a 

Hay Fllision subdivision on 
Thousand Oaks Drive. 
The joint meeting is set for 'J p.m. Tuesday at CWB offices. 
It will he the first agenda item of CWB s regular monthly meeting. 
.Also on the Tuesday agenda are discussions of the proposed 
CWB purchase of the Fox Hun 
Water Co. in Northeast Bexar 
County and acquisition of a land site for a proposed $3511 million water treatment plant.

City-building a big job that needs care 

Planning, it is obvious, is a conscious decision on how to use one’s resources. 
City planning, now in contro­ versy in San Antonio, is a public decision on how to use public resources: Water, natural gas, roads, utilities, taxes and ser­ vices. 
The City of San A ntonio’s 
Planning Commission, chaired by 
M argaret 
L ee/n cr, has evolved growth sk etch es for planning purposes and the plan­ ning process is coming to public understanding. 
There is pressure building in key areas of the planning process and some pressures are m asquerading with the result that some of the public debate may well be m is­ understood. 
At any rate. City Council will eventually decide how far and how firm its planning decisions will be. Council holds important but not all of the options. 
The growth sketch approach to planning is sound. Whenever public funds are to be invested or public interests to be served 
— or protected — public policy decisions becom e the over-riding concern. 
The inevitable pressure for the city to push northward has run smack against strong voter concern for the fate of the city's water supply. Whatever one’s view s about growth in the 

 aquifer's recharge zone of sen­ sitive geologic formations, there seem s little room to challenge the search for expert guidance on risks. 
And water is but one public concern. 
When the energy crunch lands with expected im* pact of either cost or regulation, a city's efficiency will depend upon how well it meets its en­ ergy requirem ents. That in­ cludes electric power, natural gas and gasoline. Sprawl be­ comes costly for all concerned. 
The far-reaching water, sewer and electric lines need to be economized. 
Public in­ vestment in streets, police and fire protection, sanitation and the other services deserve en­ lightened public understanding through debate. 
City Council has taken for­ ward-looking action to find comprehensive answers to the hard questions. Not all the answers are in and the planning process shouldn’t be needlessly rushed under pressure. The en­ gineering study by Metcalf & 
Eddy will becom e invaluable inform ation for the city for years to come. Capital require­ ments, through bond issues, will have to be projected on the basis of service extensions and antici­ pated revenues. 
The job is big. It deserves to be treated that way. 
Joint tax appraisal service still in formative stages 

The City-County Appraisal 
Office is still m oving toward proving its u sefuln ess as a m ore-efficient and effective public service. 
The City Board of Equaliza­ tion filed a sharp critique with 
City Council at the end of its work session a few weeks ago. 
Last week. 
County 
Tax 
A ssessor-C olleetor Ben Shaw outlined his version of an effec­ tive charter for CCA. He at­ tached an Attorney G eneral’s opinion that holds the assessor* collector must have some con­ trol over appraisals used in the county assessing process. 
Shaw’s charter proposals in­ volve debatable items. He pro­ poses the CCA board hire both the administrator and a chief appraiser and he proposes co­ 

 chairmen to head the board and to-rotate from meeting to meet­ ing in the chair. 
He explains the dual leader­ ship proposal as “adding to the checks and b a la n c e s.” Co­ chairm an operation seem s needlessly clumsy. 
Lack of a single boss pre­ sents greater problems. 
It seems obvious that the asses- sor-collector should be on the board and 
Shaw proposes membership for the city’s tax chief or finance director, plus m em bers from other taxing agencies (school districts, etc.) that join CCA. 
Reorganization of the board and refinement of the charter should get careful deliberation. 
The service should be nudged toward professional status. 
Big noise from a small gun 

The Hawks and the Doves will count votes Wednesday in the U. S. Senate. At issue is the nomination of Paul Warnke as 
President Carter's arms limita­ tion negotiator and director of the 
Arms 
Control and 
Disarmament Agency. 
Warnke should w in. 

He should win because of his experience and willingness to try to break the sterile balance of terror. Warnke has drawn concentrated opposition of the 

Calderon praised 

Hawks and has been accused of wanting to disarm America uni­ laterally. That accusation is false but the apostles of fear keep hammering at it. 
Last week, the anti-Warnke drive went into high gear. A direct-mail business of Richard 
A. Yiguerie’s sent a half million postcards (with an opposition m essage) to be returned to a senator. V’ig u er ie’s effort — lobbying and fund raising — is expected to tail as it so richly deserves to do. 

Sir 
Re: Young Women's Christian 
Association. 
It was dedication day, Feb. 26, for a new YWCA facility at .KIO Castroville 
Rd. 
There were words of praise for so< many; there were empty chairs where' the dignitaries and the VIPs were sitting 
In the standing audience, on the outside looking in. was a person who has contributed a lot to the communi­ ty. 
No doubt he was contemplating the unbelievable panorama with mixed emotions, glad the dream was being realized and yet disappointed that no one would ask him to sit much less say a few words. 
That person is Manuel H. Calderon, the man many of us there knew was responsible for introducing the YWCA project for approval to the City Council rn early 1973 while he was a council­ man. 
But that was not all. The luncheon ceremonies followed, political candi­ dates were claiming praise for the project, plaques for all kinds of recog­ nition were presented, yet no one had the courtesy to mention Mr. Calderon's contribution to the project. 
Eddie Alejandro, 
Felix R. Ybarra, 
Las Palmas Town Council 

No views for unwanted child 

Sir; Roddy Stinson’s column (Feb. 
17. “ Politicos clobber kids and send taxpayers the bill” ) exposes a shame­ ful facet of the abortion issue: the unwanted children iii this world aren’t provided for. 

Most ol those kids. for whom it is necessary to have juvenile courts and facilities to cure for their needs, wouldn't lie here if a legal abortion had been readily available. 

An abortion may be immoral but it is barbaric to have children neglected, abused and starved in an affluent society. 

Those protesting legal abortions, including churchmen, offer no pro­ grams. or personal commitment to alleviate the problem of unwanted children. In other words, they would only expand the problem. 
Alex I). Broll, the anti-abortion champion iii this column, should tell us what lie. personally, is doing to solve the problem. What taxes he may be paying are not getting the job done. 
Ile. in a snide way, has previously reiected the statistics concerning these kids. 
Ile and the others of the same per­ suasion should admit there is some truth in published statistics, and prove th e ir sin c e rity as ad v o c a te s of unlimited birth, by individually giving a foster home to a half dozen or so of those unfortunates the politicians are ignoring 
Stinson deserves congratulations for being s«» frank. We don’t get much news reporting of this type, except from Paul Thompson.

Sir: Eldridge Cleaver, of all people, will soon be spreading the good word tor the Southern Baptists in a series of 
TY commercials. 
( leaver, form er civil rights fire­ brand. has apparently rejected his 
Communist ideology in favor of the 
Southern Baptist way of life. 
The breathtaking incongruity of a former* Black Panther like Cleaver accepting SB Christianity can be seen if we look ut what the SBs really stand for. 
For example, the Rev. Bruce Ed­ wards resigned recently as pastor of 

President Carter s hometown church. the Plains Baptist Church, because of a white backlash against bis pro-inte­ gration stand. 
The Plains. Cia.. Baptists staunchly reaffirmed their 11-year-old resolution banning “ Negroes aud other civil rights agitators” from church wor­ ship. 
Rev. Edwards also drew considera­ ble criticism and condemnation for the adoption of his 1-year-old Polynesian son. Phillip Daniel. 
So here we have Eldridge Cleaver louting the Southern Baptists whose 
God bates Polynesians. Negroes, and other civil right activists and loves only conservative, Caucasian Chris­ tians. 
Cleaver’s con-version is probably not so much Uncle Tomism as it is a 
“ foxhole” conversion meant to help ease him out of his current legal difficulties. 
Remember Charles “ Chuckles” Col­ son, former Nixon hatchet man. who vowed to walk over his own grand­ mother to re-elect Nixon? He “ found it” after bis legal travails started. 
Who is next on the Celebrity Save- 
Your-Soul Sweepstakes, G. Gordon 
Biddy? FII believe there's a God when 
Richard Nixon “ finds it" and comes clean.

WASHINGTON - The Bus- stuns are sneering at President 
Carter’s defense of human rights, as if it were a dreary 
Sunday school lesson out, of 
Plains, Ga. 
But it is much more serious than that. 
Carter is touching something much d«*epcr in the American character. 
I he Russians should read I hornus 
.Jefferson on the Rights of Man, 
Abraham Lincoln on the universal meaning of America, Woodrow Wil­ son and the two Roosevelt on the 
New Freedom , the New Deal, the 
Square Deal aud the dream of a de­ cent world order. 
Occasional 
And then |»erhaps ihey may under­ stand thai Jimmy Carter tuts merely gone buck to the old lessons of a country that doesnt quilt* believe in its old ideals but believes in believ­ ing 

The human rights qi lion, Inn

P re sid e n t, Jody Pow ell and the s(Mikesmen of the State Department. 
They have revived a fundamental them e of A m erican life, but they sound a little preachy, and need a different platform to be effective. 
Th** Sov lids have signed the 
Charter of the United Nations. It is a legal international treaty. It insists on human rights. They have signed it and proclaim that they keep their promises 
They have also signed the Helsinki agreem ent on human rights which established international standards of freedom, travel, and exchange of information. 
Biblical nation

(tie modern dilemma 
Hear echoes

. . I see tile as an unavoidable waste and curable confusion . , . their disorder of effort.

Carter has some friends and sup­ porters who are trying to help him in this objective. 
Ills ambassador to the United Na­ tions. Andrew Young, is trying to persuade him that he can make more progress for human rights through the United Nations than by denounc­ ing the Russians with statements out of Washington.

 gests. is an international standard of the decencies of life.

Senses questions

American people. 
Like ll 
G, W ells, lie is realty bringing the issue of human rights into the upon aud into the world.

Real war for KRA actually is over 

WASHINGTON — Fem inist backers of the Equal Rights 
Amendment have lost in the arena of public opinion. 
They have failed to convince the country of the necessity or wisdom of what they wish to do to the U.S. Con­ stitution. 
The evidence is everywhere. 
Last month, in an em barrassingly slanted ABC “ documentary” booming 
ERA, opponents — who include Sen. 
Sam Ervin and Gov. Ronald Reagan — were variously described as ultra-con­ servatives, far rightists and Commun­ ists. 

This is the politics of desperation. 
When your case is strong, your a r­ gum ents persuasive, your cause ad­ vancing, you do not savage the opposi­ tion with slanders and smears. 

Losing battle 

ERA's failure to win over America was driven home more poignantly last week during the am endm ent’s losing battle in the North Carolina Senate. 
State Sen. Bobby Lee Combs, a min­ ister. who had pledged that he would vote against ERA. was heading into the 
Senate to make good on his word. 
“ My people are two-to-one against it,” he said. 
Then, no less a personage man trie 
President of the United States got on the phone directly to cajole the Rev. 
Combs to break faith with the people. 
"I put my head down and cried,” said the senator. “ It was the P re si­ dent." 

Didn’t break 

But Sen. Combs didn’t break faith. 
He voted the way he had promised the people of western North Carolina he would vote — and ERA went down to defeat. 
For this writer, that incident pretty much sums it up. 
When a proposal has popular sup­ port. you do not need a president of the 
United States tw isting the a rm s of state legislators. 
This shameful episode reflects no credit whatsoever on the President. lf P re s id e n t C a rte r w ishes to squander the moral authority of his great office, there is no better way than to use his prestige persuading state legislators to betray their consti­ tuents and their consciences, on the most important vote many will ever cast. 

More evidence 

Nor is this the only evidence ERA's support is eroding. 
Even its most avid adherents must concede that the more widely it has been debated, the more broadly it has been discussed, the smaller its base of support. 
In 1972, ERA easily sw ept both houses of Congress. 
Before the year was out 22 states had ratified. 

Eight more followed in 1973; three more in 1974 
With five years to go and only five 
.Mates left, ratification seemed a cer­ tainty. 
T hen, how ever, people began to study the matter more closely, to take a second look at what they were about to do on the Constitution. 
From that day forward, ERA has been in trouble. 

Ratifications 

Since 1974, two states have ratified the Amendment; but three others have rescinded earlier ratification. 
Despite th** relentless propaganda of the Big Media, the boiler room opera­ tions run out of the Ford and Curter 
White House, progress has proceeded at a snail’s pace. 
Iii 1975, when the people of New J e r­ sey and New York were given an op­ portunity to vote statewide on ERA. the former rejected it by a clear m a­ jority, and New York, perhaps the most liberal state iii America, sent it down to defeat by a crushing 4oo.(MKI votes. 

ERA does nut. today, have anything approaching the broad public support the Forefathers wanted for un amend­ ment — which would alter that found­ ing document of this republic 

Only hope 

ERA s only hope now is, through arm -D u stin g , log-rolling aud p re si­ dential power politics, to ram FRA through three more slate legislatures 
Which a bad way to go about changing the 1 s t «»ns>t it ut ton. 
As tor that gathering majority which opposes ERA, they will have to pros that - in the state legislatures of ii- limits and M issouri, O klahom a and

Naval officer is a creature of_ Capitol HUL and Pentagon 
To m ost, Rickover is admired or hated 

Page .11 -A 

Adm. Hymati (i. Rickover is known as the “ Father of the Nu­ clear Submarine." 

Over the years, he bas become one of the most controversial men in the Navy’s history. 

This is the second of a four-part series about the admiral. 

 sea power which some officers believe is superior to the United States. 
The task of reb u ildin g a surface fleet, allowed to age during the Viet­ nam war. and the ( arter Administra­ tio n 's c o m m itm e n t lo cut defense spending have made the nuclear Navy a momentous issue again 
For the price of the nuclear-powered cruiser, which President C arter eli­ minated from his budget, for example, the Navy could have several conven­ tionally powered escort ships — though they admittedly would not last as long as vessels powered by atomic reac tors. 
Important fight 

As Rickover heads into his most im ­ portant fight since the struggle over nuclear submarines in the 195os, there are signs of weakening in his power base. 
Many of the congressional elders who considered Rickover one of their own are gone from Capitol Hill. The 
Joint Atomic Energy Committee, the adm iral’s political home port over the 

 years, has been dismantled after pro­ longed buffeting by nuclear power cri­ tics. 
There also has been a basic change in the n a tu re of C o n g ress, with younger in d e p e n d e n t-m inded m e m b e rs g a in in g in c re a sin g influence. 
Then there is the Carter Adminis­ tration's planned reorganization of the federal energy bureaucracy 
The latter is of concern to Rickover, since he is both the nuclear power chief in the Navy’s Sea Systems Com­ mand and director of Naval Reactors for the civilian Energy Research and 
Development Administration. 
Early days 

The dual role goes back to the early days of R ic k o v e r's work on naval reactors. For many years before crea­ tion of tin* Energy Research and De­ velopment Administration, he was an official of the Atomic Energy Com­ mission at the sam e tim e he was a 
Navy flag officer. 

The a r r a n g e m e n t p re se n te d him with infinite opportunities for short cu ts b ecau se he w as able to do business with him self, to m ake d e ­ mands on one of his own offices and respond from the other, without get­ ting lip from his desk. 
Now. besides watching out for the shifting sands within g ov ern m en t, 
Rickover finds himself at the center of a pitched battle between the Navy and its three prime shipbuilders over cost overruns. 

Three companies claim that because of inflation, changed orders, late deli­ very of government equipment, Rick­ over interference, and one thing and another, the governm ent owes them about $2.5 billion for work alread y done 
Holding out 
R ickover. in sistin g m an y of the claim s are fraudulent, has held out against settlement plans. 
T here have been ch arg es that he wants to nationalize the private shipyards. 
Meanwhile, he continues to be criti­ cized as being too conservative in his reactor engineering over the years and for not pressing hard enough for deve­ lopm ent of s m a lle r, lig h te r power plants 
And because he is a power unto himself and because he rolls on year in and year out without any sign of pre­ paring a successor, his nonadm ircrs liken him to the late FBI Director J. 
Edgar Hoover. 
But whatever faults Rickover’s cri­ tics find and whatever naval policy the new administration follows. Carter is among those who regard him as an extraordinary leader. 
In a telephone interview, the Presi­ dent d escribed R ickover as a m an whose “ adherence to principle is ab­ solute.” a man who "as much as any­ one in our country provided us with leadership during the cold war years.’’

A R G U IN G HIS points before a joint congressional com ­ mittee is Adm . H ym an G. Rickover. This w as in 1975 w hen he w as in a dispute w ith U.S. Steel over the firm 's refusal to furnish steel for the Trident subm arine p ro­ gram . 

Rickover was largely responsible for dragging the Navy into the nuclear age against its will, and most of his critics concede that the United S tates d e ­ ployed atomic subm arines years e a r­ lier than it otherwise would have be­ cause of his determination. 
But today the Navy faces a massive modernization program that could cost far more than the Apollo moon landing program and could reach as high as 
$8o bilHon. 
The U.S. fleet is down to half the size 
U was in the years after World War ll. and the Sox let Union has grown into a 

To most, he was either held in unu­ sual esteem or hated with a special passion. • 
From the time Congress helpc»d him force the nuclear submarine on an un­ willing Navy and fend off retirement, 
Rickover has been as much a creature of Capitol Hill as the Pentagon. 
No other m ilitary officer has ever played politics so well and so long in uniform. 
"I worked for five secretaries of de­ fense," said a recently retired adm ir­ al. “ and every year they would decide, 
‘This is the year to get Rickover.’ They would put their lawyers to work on it. but they never got anywhere.”

Narrow walks? 

Why can't the sidewalks downtown be wider? 
There is hardly any room to walk on them at certain times of the day. 
I think the worst ease of this is at Alamo Plaza where they had a chance to make some more room tor people walking down the street and just put back the narrow, skinny, sidewalks that only have enough room for two people to pass without interfering with one another. 
Is there some problem here? 
Why can't the city include wider walks on all its projects to make being downtown more comfortable and enjoyable? 
.H ST WONDERING 

U W ID E S ! 
DowTiToucn 
SIDCWALKS it 

We have asked that question ourselves about new buildings built in the downtown area. 
The city has a m ini­ mum sidewalk width of three feet. 
All the sidewalks around new buildings and around 
Alamo Plaza meet that minimum width. 
We do think the city needs to update those minimum standards, however. In areas with high pedestrian traffic, three foot wide sidewalks are com pletely inade­ quate. 
At Alamo Plaza, how­ ever, a lot of people will now he walking through, 

 rather than around, the plaza. 
The stoned-over plaza area is suitable for pe­ destrian traffic, so the sidewalks are not that important. There are a couple of places, how­ ever, where the sidewalk on the west side of the plaza gets very narrow. lake we said, the city probably ought to give a look at the ordinance on minimum sidewalk widths and see if it doesn't need updating. 
It's going to be pretty congested in front of one of those new hotels if there is only a three-foot wide sidewalk. 

Liquor ads on TV 

Can you explain to mc why liquor ads for vodka and other hard drinks are shown on KWEX-TV.and they are not shown on any of the other stations in San 
Antonio? 
Isn t this against the law or something? 
W ATCHER 

Contrary to popular opinion, no. it is not against the law or any 
Federal Communications 
Commission regulation or anything else for hard liquor to be advertised on television. 
“ It is just an unwritten rule." said a spokesman from K W EX. 

The three commercial television stations in San 
\ntonio all belong to the 
National 
Assn. of 
Broadcasters. The hard liquor ads are discour­ aged bv the television code tf the NAB. 

K W E X belongs to the 
Spanish International 
Network and does not subscribe to the Tele­ vision Code of the Na­ 

 tional Assn. of Broadcas­ ters. 
K W EX , however, does not have any local ads for liquor running, we were told. 
Rather, what you are seeing are ads which ori­ ginate in Mexico. K W EX picks up 24 hours of 
Mexican television each week. The commercials are picked up along with the programs, so occa­ sionally, a vodka ad will be included along with other Mexican products being sold via commer­ cials. 
Unlike the ban on c i­ garette advertising on TV 
— which is mandated by law — the ban on liquor ads is really not a legal matter. 
CatliKh ads 

I see those catfish restaurant ads are running on television and in the newspapers again. 
I thought there was some lawsuit against this firm for not serving catfish? 
What has happened? 
Is this just another case of a big firm violating the law again? What is the story here? 
CONFUSED 
Obviously, the restaur­ ant is now serving catfish instead of turbot as al­ leged by the consumer protection division of the 
Texas Attorney General's cilice. 
The restaurant was or­ dered to pay a $3.(NMI tine in a judgment entered bv 
Hist. Court Judge Fred shannon late last year. 
The judge had ordered the restaurant to stop advertising catfish un­ less it is actually serving rattish

Edgewood eyes pre-school fate 

Edgewood School Dis­ trict trustees are expect­ ed to decide next week whether to keep 
4-year-olds at the Jose 
Angel Cardenas Early 
Childhood Center — or to move them. 
Supt. Hu ben Lopez re­ commended moving them to campuses closer to their homes at a Mon­ day board meeting. 
His recommendation also called for making the Cardenas center a school for the severely handicapped. 
Parents 
— led by 
Minnie Aleman - - have protested the efforts to relocate the more than 
Job pre-school students. 
They also protested 
Monday. 
No provisions 

Their complaints in­ clude charges that the school has made no pro­ visions to move the pre-school students. 

Supt. Lttpez said the issue is who's goin g to use the Cardenas Center 

— not who is goin g to he educated 

Mrs. 
Aleman, who served on a committee to study the feasbility of moving the pre­ schoolers, also says the district has enough fund ing to cover the needs of the handicapped without moving the preschoolers. 
Lopez said Monday that the changes are be­ ing recommended so the district will be in com­ pliance with a new fe­ deral law for educating the mentally, emotional­ ly and physically handi­ capped. 
Judy Lozano, director of special education ser­ vices for the Edgewood 
School District, said 
Monday there have been misunderstandings in that community that the 

 education of preschoolers would be terminated. 
She said that wasn't true. 
Lopez also said Mon­ day that the issue is who’s going to use the 
Cardenas ( enter — not who is going to be edu­ 

 cated. Lopez said both groups will be educated by the district. 
A feasibility study on how the district should comply with the Taw for the handicapped was re­ commended by Mrs. Lo­ zano in August. 
Since that time, a citi­ zens' committee studied the situation and a public hearing has been held. 
The trustees are ex­ pected to reach a de­ cision at next Monday's board meeting.

Lackland teacher is new TSTA leader 

MKS. V IR G IN IA 
STACEY 
.. wins state post 

By V IC K IE DAVIDSON 
This must be the era of unexpected winners 
Jimmy ( arter came from an obscure town in Georgia to the presidency. 
Virginia Stacey came from an obscure school district to the presidency of the 
Texas State Teachers* Assn. 
"It is very unusual for a person from 
Lackland School District with its 57 members to win.” admitted Mrs. Sta­ cey Monday. 
Election 
Sin* won a runoff election to head the 
14o.Of IO-mom Imt teachers organization. 
TSTA is the largest teacher organiza­ tion iii the state. 
Mrs. Staley, an English teacher at Lackland High School, won with about a six per cent lead over an Abi­ lene administrator. 
The vote was 42.658 to 37.781. . 
"I thought it would be closer,” Mrs. 

Stacey said Monday alter the results were announced. 
"I won because of all the help I got from this area." 
Leader 
Mrs. Stacey, who’s been an outspo­ ken leader for bettering teachers working environment and bettering education, will become president-elect of the organization iii June. 
She ll take the reins of the presi­ dency June 1878 
Mrs Stacey. 48. says she run for the 
1878 post because the legislature will be in session then. "The legislature is where everything for quality education hap|lens in this state." she said 
Monday Mrs. Stacey said it's still too soon to tell what the specific issues will lie then — or what she’ll be working toward. 
"But,” she said. "I'm going to put my heart and soul in it.”

‘M AD W O M A N OF JERUSALEM/ WITHOUT SCARF, 
...chats with women in courtyard at Yod Lakashish 
E lderly fin d th eir niche 

9 in Jeru salem 

J E R U S A L E M - In Israel she is called the Mad Woman of Jerusalem. 
Her name is Myriam Mendilow, a sabra (native Israeli) and once a top a d m in istra to r in the city 's schools, who resigned 14 years ago to create a prominent place for the aged in Israel’s youth-oriented cul­ ture. 
Not a place of isolation, hut one where young and old would cele­ brate holidays together and would share their life experiences with one another. 
It was to be more than an after­ noon social club, where the old play parlor gam es until it is time to go back to their empty rooms or the doorsteps and alleyways of .Jerusa­ lem’s streets. 
It was to be a project that would bring back feelings of self-worth and the confidence and indepen­ dence of their youth. 
The educator wanted them to feel that they were contributors in their society, not burdens to their fa ­ milies. chanty cases to their neighbors. 

Last in a series 

“ They are rich because they have something to do with their time. 
Every citizen has the right to be a part of a community, to enjoy pro­ ductivity,” Mrs. Mendilow s u n s . 
Israel in 19H2 had lx*t*» a nation for only 14 years. It needed .Noting, self-sufficient citizens who could develop industrial and agricultural enterprises. 
And above all it needed strong men and women to serve in the 
Israeli Army. The major priority ot this pocket-sized nation was survival— to defend its right of existence. 
“ I had to introduce my govern­ ment to the problems of the old. It w as creatin g two co m m u n ities—those who were wealthy and happy and those who were not." she recalls. 
“ What’s all this I asked? The community is healthy when one is responsible for tin* other! 
“ We must bridge the gap between generations, between those who are wealthy and those who are not. 
"We must be responsible for those who have n ot," she continues iii a irm articulate monologue. 
And so she resigned from the 

By 
NANCY 
SCOTT JONES 

 school system and founded Yad 
Lakashish (Lifeline for the Old), a private project where the handi­ capped. the aged and the destitute many of them beggars — could come to work each day to earn their livings. 
“ What does it mean to teach reading and writing?" she asks mb. 
“ Ah. but to teach human values 

"I could not teach my children respect for the old and the aged, when they were sitting on doorsteps and waiting for death. 
"It was a joke!" she says. 
Today there are more than 45U men and women w ho come five days a week to work in the 13 workshops at Yad Lakashish. 
Their motto is "God give me work while I live and life while I work." 
There are sabras. European and 
Middle 
Eastern Jew s in their 
\ an n uIkes. Russian immigrant wo­ men in their print babushkas. Arab 
Muslims and Christians. 
Complex 
They are artists, bookbinders, weavers, seamstresses, cardboard box makers, potters, teachers and cooks who come to the neat complex til stone buildings ut 14 Shivte Israel 
(Tribes of Israel) Street. 

Shivte Israel runs through the 
Musrara quarter of Jerusalem — a no-man’s land, which until the Six 
Day War separated the Old City from the modern capital of Israel. 

I had gone to interview My nam 
Mendilow al the suggestion of Milo- mo Hanoch, un eighth generation 
Israeli now iii San Antonio, where he heads youth education aud family services at the Jewish Community 
Center.

SAN ANTONIO EXPRESS— Tuesday March 8 I 977 
Bringing young to the old 

But when I went to Yad Lakdshish on an early Sunday morning, there was no time for a lengthy, private interview with this small, handsome woman. 
To catch this 2oth Century humanist, who has the same relentless energy of a dedicated ballerina is not a small feat. 
She is never resting — always help­ ing volunteers or chatting with the old workers, stopping.to give each one a singular kiss, a warm hug. an attentive 
(‘ar. 
Snark 
Seldom does she touch her mid-morn mg coffee and snack of sweets brought to lier desk by one of the workers 

 rills morning she was conducting a tour of young school g i r l s who had come to see the aged at work and to greet her friend from Washington. 
D.C., Janice K. Blumberg. 

Mrs. Blumberg. w de of the president of B'nai B ’rith in the I .S., comes to 
Israel at least twice a year On each visit she buys the exquisite gifts made by the old at Yad Lakashisli to sell iii the B'nai B'rith Museum s gift shop iii' 
Washington 

The goods—woven linens, ceramic beads, beautifully knit sweaters and mufflers, festive yarmulkes, yarn dolls and puppets are sold iii Yad Laka- shish's gift shop. the Bider Craftsman, as well as in the posh boutiques of Is­ rael's high-rise hotels. 

Explaining the work of the old to the young is an important part of Mrs. 
Mendilow’s philosophy. 

Students who visit Yad Lakashisli are required to write compositions about the center for Mrs. Mendilow. Her desk is piled with bulging manila folders of their essays 

“ We must bring the young toward the old.” she tells me as she leads the children to the bookbindery, where sin* explains to them in Hebrew what these men are doing. 

 will pick you up here and take you to teach others to make the bags.” 
A schizophrenic man came to Yad 
Lakashisli. but refused to work. 
Then after six months, he made handpainted tiles, but the scenes were grotesque—a three-faced man. “ It s because I do not feel as one person,” he explained. 
Another one showed a huge foot crushing a man s back. It was made after an angry quarrel with Mrs. Men­ dilow. “ Don’t tread on me,” he warned. 
Now there is a picture from the man every morning on her desk But the sketching is precise, and the colors are light— rolling landscapes in wheat and earth tones and delicate pen and inks of 
Jerusalem by night. 
. Then there is the woman who came to the center with only the tatooed Nazi concentration camp serial number for identification. 
Still another Nazi victim, a woman whose mind is completely gone, hides under the table every.time a plane flies overhead. 
Yet, she is able to make handsome hammered brass bookends. 

 the workshops. Yad a meals on wheels center and 

$ I per day 

(rerontolopv 

“ Today they speak about geronto­ logy, but when I began this, there was nothing,” she continues. “ The more the young were developed, the less they looked at the old ” 

But Mrs Mendilow persisted until they were looking at the old, and along the way she gained the reputation as the “ Mad Woman of Jerusalem.” 

She took her project s proposal and the needs of the elderly to the Ministry of Social Welfare, but they had other priorities and thin budgets 

So she funded Yad Lakashisli from her own pocket. 

Her husband, chairman of the Fng- lish Department at Hebrew University, 

ELDERLY G E N T L E M A N 
...rebinds books gave the elderly bookbinders his stu­ dents' books to repair. It was their first job. 

Now Yad Lakashisli is recognized by the Ministry of Social Welfare and is given some financial assistance. 
Approximately 25 per cent of its in­ come comes from government sub­ sidies, 35 per cent from private contri­ butions and 4(1 per cent from the sale of products made by the workers. 
Israel’s schools now accept Yad 
Lakashish’s bid to repair tin* children's textbooks. 
“ Your book has been repaired by the old people of Jerusalem,” is stamped 

 iii every bound copy. 
There is a waiting list of elderly people who want to be a part of this vital community. 

Each worker is paid for his work, IO liras per day, a little more than $1. 
Their salary is the fruit of their labors, says Mrs. Mendilow 
“ They are not spoon-fed,” she says with pride. 
“ They take the bus; they wash and put on a clean shirt to come to work, to have their cup of tea.” 
The old peoples’ salaries are supple­ mented with small, monthly pensions from the government's 
National 
Insurance. Those who worked may also receive retirement pay. 
The Israeli government has deve­ loped similiar programs to Yad Laka- shish. They are primarily social clubs financed by municipal and national government funds. 
But Yad Lakashisli is the only pro­ ject. where the elderly receive an in­ come for their work, says a worker in the Department of Visiting Foreign 

Journalists. 
Iii addition to 
Lakashish runs program for those at the those who are homebound. 
Nutritious food—fresh soups, fish, breads and fruit—are prepared daily in the ((‘liter's immaculate kitchen by a woman who is losing her eyesight. 
A health clinic is also av ailable at the 
(•(‘liter. Dental care, at reduced rates. is available three times a week and a physician conies weekly to give routine check-ups. 

Showers 

Every Friday the workers who have no bathroom facilities in their homes may use the showers at Yad Lakashish. 
There are washing machines where they may launder their clothes. 
Special outings to parks and mu­ seums are offered, and the trips create new ideas in their.art work. 
Unglazed ceramic beads in modern chokers and simple necklaces were made after the artists saw the artifacts found al Massada. a Herodian fortress where Jews committed suicide rather than surrender to the Romans in 73 
AD. 
Volunteers—young men and women—come to discuss international and national events with the elder craftsmen. 
(jilts are made for school children for special holidays At Purim, the young and the old dance together. 
This is just the beginning for My nam 
Mendilow's Yod Lakashish. 
A building—built with tissue-thin walls — is being renovated for more workshops and a social area. 
She hopes to have a roof garden where the men and women can work and talk in the sunshine. The view from the roof overlooks their beloved Jeru­ salem. 
The Mad Woman of Jerusalem ’s philosophy about growing old is posi­ tive. 
“ It is the last station in life. It is not meant to be the end. but to be a con­ tinuation of life at a slower pace.”

Early days 

But it was not always that way. Iii the early days of Yad Lakashish. it was 
Mrs. Mendilow who did the pleading. 
She would stop beggars on the street. 
“ What did you do for a living?’” she asked a woman begging for money in the Old City. 
“ Nothing.” replied the woman. 
“ Surely you can do something,” Mrs. 
Mendilow pressed. 
"Well. I can weave shopping bags,” offered the beggar woman. 
“ Good, tomorrow you are a teacher.” Mrs Mendilow told her. "I

Calcium aids bone density 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: Can you explain loss of bone density and just how calcium is involved in it? How can we older women avoid it? I understand it can be a problem after menopause begins. — Mrs. L.O. 
Bones consist of a kind of honeycomb pattern of tiny spaces. These holes are continaully filled in with calcium .’ The calcium helps give bones their remarkable strength, allowing weightlifters to lift tons of weight without collapsing under it. 
But the calcium isn’t permanent. It is forever being used up and re ­ placed. When more cal­ cium is taken away than is replaced, hone fragi­ lity results. An X-ray can determine a bone’s relative brittleness, this 
“ lack of density.” 
Some thinning (osteo­ porosis) is a result of aging iii either males or fem ales. 
Sin ce ho r­ mones seem to be in ­ volved in the business of laving down calcium in bones, m enopause, as you say, can cause it in women 
However, lack of protein and calcium in the diet can be factors as well. Bones also need exercise to maintain their strength. Much of the menopause problem may result from the lessened activity of middle age. 
Unused bones become weak bones. Sam e with muscles 

B .M .R tests 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: Please explain the “ B M R . ” test, the one where von breathe through a tube? — 
P H . 
This stands for "basal metabolism rate” test. It is not used so much these days because of new and better methods for evaluating thyroid gland func­ tion. With the B M R , one breathes in oxygen through a tube, then breathes into a special chamber. The amount of oxygen used up gives p pretty fair indic­ tation of how efficiently the body's energy is being 
"burned up.” 
A newer test, called " P B I.” for “ protein bound iodine” is more exact because it is a more direct measurement of gland production. It requires only a sm all sam ple of blood for test purposes. Hither procedure can be done in a doctor’s office. 
Dear Dr. Thosteson: What does the term "cardiac shock” mean? — E .J. 
Shock is any disruption of blood circulation. It is referred to generally as circulatory shock. The re­ sult is inadequate blood supply to body tissues. 
Cardiac shock is that caused by a defective heart. 
Stroke (inadequate circulation to the brain) is a form of shock. 

To find out wha! causes high blood pressure and what con be done to treat it, send for o copy of “ Conrtrolling Your Blood 
Pressure (Hypertension)" by Dr. Thosteson. W rite to him in core of this newspaper, enclosing a long, self-addressed, stomped envelope and 35 cents. .

The coalition is com ­ posed of six representa­ tives and one senator who have introduced le­ g islatio n fo rcin g la n d ­ lords to comply with re­ q u irem en ts of housing codes affecting health and safety. 

The House m e m b e rs are sponsoring HB 845, under which a “ Warrant of H abitability” would become part of the Texes code. The Senate version does the same. 

Under it. the landlord would be ob ligated to co m ply with r e q u ir e ­ m en ts of a p p lic a b le housing codes materially 

 affecting health and s a ­ fety. 
Landlords also would he required to maintain rental units in a safe and habitable condition and provide appropriate and necessary services such a s w a te r, p lu m b in g , e le ctricity , san itatio n , locks and weather pro­ tection. 
The tenant would be required to use the pre­ m ises in a reaso n able m anner considering the purpose for which it was designated and intended. 
Failure of the landlord to comply with the w ar­ rant of habitability would allow the tenant the fol­ lowing rem edies under the law: 
• Rent reduction and, 
• 
The rights to ter­ m inate the agreem en t with return of security deposit, or 
• lf the cost of repair is not greater than one month's rent, then the tenant m ay rep air the 

 defect or have it repaired and deduct the cost, or 
• Be entitled to collect in court actual dam ages, attorney’s fees and a civil penalty.

Police 
Asn. sells 
C-W show tickets 

The San Antonio Police Officers Assn. is conduct­ ing a telephone solicitation drive to sell tickets for a sum m er country-western concert at the Municipal 
Auditorium. 
The upcoming music show, the only fundraising event of the year for the association, is scheduled for 
June 26, Al Peeler, president of the association said. 
Part of the proceeds from the event will support the association’s pledge of a $5oo monthly donation to the Mental Health/Mental Retardation unit at 227 
W. Drexel Ave. 
All telephone solicitors are hired from the po­ lice-sponsored Explorer post. No money is paid to any promotional group, Peeler said. tick ets for the concert, priced at $5 for adults and 
$3 for children, can be purchased by calling 22H-1214.

Work rights 

AUSTIN - Three dif- ferent m easures calling on C o n gress to oppose repeal of Section 14b of the 1947 Taft-Hartley Act w ere se n t to s u b c o m ­ mittee here Monday by the House State Affairs 
Committee. 

All three m easures cull on Congress to retain the so-called ‘‘right-to-work ’ 

Capitol briefs 

KCOR, 24 happy hours daily of great music, news, 

 dramas, sports, commentaries, and community service 

135o on the Dial 

MTS' 

 law option to the indivi­ dual states. 
The three resolutions were introduced by Rev. 
Ja m e s Now lin of San An­ tonio, Clay Smothers of 
Dallas, and Gibson Lewis of Fort Worth.

British farm leader asks 
U.S. to open its markets 

SHELLY KIGGANS 
. , . she’s safe 
Gunman releases 
I hostage 

By BIM , CUNNINGHAM 
AGRICULTURE EDITOR 
“ European farmers believe that in return for taking your foodstuffs, you should be prepared to open your mar­ kets to the dairy and meat products produced from them.” Sir Henry 
Plumb, president of the Farmers 
Union of England and Wales, said here 
Monday. 
Sir Henry was addressing delegates of the Diamond Jubilee Convention of the National Farmers Union at the 
Convention Center. 
The English leader said members of the European Economic Community 
(the Common Market) will be ready to meet American farmers half way on moves designed to bring about ex­ panded outlets for farm products on both sides of the Atlantic. 
He called for members of the 
Farmers Union to work together to see that governments at long last put into effect the plans within a national agri­ cultural stability. 
“ The trading relationship between the United States and the EEC is of immense importance on both sides of the Atlantic as the community imports 

'Each o f us (ll.S. and Europe) has a responsibility to sec some order is brought into international trade iti agricultural commodities, and fo r our part we would like to see a more two-way traffic in fo o d exports ’ 

 a fifth of its agricultural requirements from the United States and is that country's largest single market,” he said. 
Pointing to the need for more ex­ ports to the United States by the EEC, he said that between 197o and 1975 U.S. exports in valued terms increased by 
17o per cent, while EEC exports to the 
U.S. increased by only 52 per cent. 
He reported that U.S. exports of farm products to the E F X in 1975 amounted to $5.B billion while U.S. imports of E E C farm products amounted to only $1 billion. 
Sir Henry said that instead of quarreling the U.S. and the Common 
Market should recognize that they are the two most important trading partners in agricultural products in 

 the world. 
“ Further, each of us has a responsi­ bility to see that some order is brought into international trade in agricultural commodities, and for our part we would like to see a more two-way traf­ fic in food exports.” 
“ We cannot have a healthy economy in the United States with agriculture — which is still the nation’s largest in­ dustry — in the bankruptcy courts,” 
U.S. Sen. John Melcher, D-Mont., told the convention Monday afternoon. 
He added that it is necessary lo see that agriculture has enough income to buy the machinery, petroleum pro­ ducts and other supplies that it needs and to pay the accumulated dc*bts in town and to create real demand, jobs and tax -paying economic activ ity.

CLEVELAND (A PI - 
A black man who said he was “ mad at the white world” early Tuesday released unharmed the teen-aged girl he had held hostage for nearly 
IO hours, exchanging her freedom for a television set. police said. 
He continued to hold a pol ice captain at gun­ point in a suburban city hall. 
Warrensville Heights 
Police Chief Craig Mer­ chant identified the gun­ man as Cory Moore. 2o. of Warrensv die Heights. 
Police Ut. Charles Tut­ tle said Moore drew a gun and fired twice after lie got into an argument while waiting in line at th** traffic bureau office in midafternoon. 
Tuttle said Moore then took as hostages police 
( apt. Leo Keglovic and 
Shelly Kiggans, 17. a high school senior who works part-time in the traffic fine waiver bur­ eau.

The senator told the group that he felt the time had come for the United 
States to agree with Canada on a min­ imum price for export sales. 
Australia lie explained that the two nations provide 7o per cent of the w heat that is sold abroad and added that Australian growers also have shown interest in joining the other two countries on a minimum world price. 
Melcher said he was for a rounded economic recovery package including fair farm prices. 
He listed as fair farm prices $4 per bushel of wheat, $3 for corn and $5o per hundred pounds for fat cattle. 
In reference to cattle he rapped U.S. policies which have allowed shipments of processed and frozen beef to be imported into the United States with­ out being included in the quota ol beet imports. 
J. O. Wright, secretary of the Sas­ katchewan WJieat Pool, said he be­ lieved that if Canada and the United 
States took the lead on a minimum sale price of wheat exported, Australia and some of the European countries would follow. 
Pleased 
, 
The Saskatchewan Wheat Pool han­ dles two thirds of the grain marketed and half the livestock marketed in the province, Wright said. 
Wright said that Canadian wheat growers were quite pleased that the two nations had recognized the prob­ lem of export prices and that they wanted to do something about it. 
. 
He suggested that minimum and maximum prices would be set in the agreement. He said he favored a rather wide range in the two prices. 
He pointed out that where the supply could be controlled, reserves could bi' established. The control would make it possible to avoid damaging price var­ iations. Wright said.

Wednesday.
